These include:\n\n1. Calixarenes: Especially the π-rich aromatic calix[4]arene and calix[6]arene derivatives, which present a bowl-shaped cavity lined with aromatic rings. These hosts can encapsulate alkali metal cations (e.g., K+, Na+) and organic cations (e.g., ammonium ions) via strong cation-π interactions.\n\n2. Cyclophanes: These are macrocyclic compounds containing two or more aromatic rings held in close proximity. Their rigid, π-rich cavities are ideal for binding cations through cation-π interactions, often with high selectivity.\n\n3. Cucurbiturils: While cucurbiturils are more famous for ion-dipole and hydrophobic interactions, certain derivatives and binding modes involve significant cation-π contributions, especially with aromatic guests or cationic species.\n\n4. Crown Ethers (Aromatic): Some crown ethers incorporate aromatic rings into their structure, enabling cation-π interactions in addition to the classic ion-dipole binding with metal cations.\n\n5. Pillararenes: These are pillar-shaped macrocycles with electron-rich aromatic walls, capable of binding cationic guests via cation-π interactions.\n\nIn all these cases, the key feature is the presence of a π-electron-rich cavity or surface that can interact favorably with cationic species. The strength and selectivity of binding depend on the size, shape, and electronic properties of both the host and the guest.\n\nReferences: For further reading, see reviews on cation-π interactions in supramolecular chemistry (e.g., Chem.
